2:23:1366:0:14:71:380:708:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:Cohort Crowding: How Resources Affect Collegiate Attainment
2:24:1425:14:14:71:215:694:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:John Bound and Sarah Turner
2:25:1452:14:14:71:232:680:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:NBER Working Paper No. 12424
2:26:1480:14:14:71:133:666:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:August 2006
2:27:1491:14:29:71:156:652:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:JEL No. I23, H52
2:28:1507:29:42:273:338:623:12:g_d0_f2:12:g_d0_f2:ABSTRACT
2:29:1515:42:14:71:539:581:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:Analyses of college attainment typically focus on factors affecting enrollment demand, including the
2:30:1615:14:15:71:539:567:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:financial attractiveness of a college education and the availability of financial aid, while implicitly
2:31:1718:15:14:71:539:552:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:assuming  that  resources  available  per  student  on  the  supply  side  of  the  market  are  elastically
2:32:1826:14:14:71:539:538:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:supplied. The higher education market in the United States is dominated by public and non-profit
2:33:1922:14:14:71:539:524:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:production, and  colleges  and universities receive considerable subsidies from state, federal, and
2:34:2021:14:14:71:539:510:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:private sources. Because consumers pay only a fraction of the cost of production, changes in demand
2:35:2120:14:14:71:539:496:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:are unlikely to be accommodated fully by colleges and universities without commensurate increases
2:36:2217:14:15:71:539:482:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:in non-tuition revenue. For this reason, public investment in higher education plays a crucial role in
2:37:2319:15:14:71:539:467:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:determining the degrees produced and the supply of college-educated workers to the labor market.
2:38:2415:14:14:71:539:453:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:Using data covering the last half of the twentieth century, we find strong evidence that large cohorts
2:39:2517:14:14:71:539:439:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:within  states  have  relatively  low  undergraduate  degree  attainment,  reflecting  less  than  perfect
2:40:2623:14:14:71:539:425:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:elasticity of supply in the higher education market. That large cohorts receive lower public subsidies
2:41:2725:14:14:71:539:411:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:per student in higher education explains this result, indicating that resources have large effects on
2:42:2826:14:14:71:539:397:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:degree  production.  Our  results  suggest  that  reduced  resources  per  student  following  from  rising
2:43:2933:14:15:71:539:383:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:cohort size and lower state expenditures are likely to have significant negative effects on the supply
2:44:3035:15:42:71:333:368:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:of college-educated workers entering the labor market.
2:45:3089:42:14:71:129:326:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:John Bound
2:46:3099:14:14:71:197:312:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:Department of Economics
2:47:3122:14:15:71:183:298:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:University of Michigan
2:48:3144:15:14:71:205:283:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:Ann Arbor, MI 48109-1220
2:49:3168:14:14:71:123:269:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:and NBER
2:50:3176:14:28:71:166:255:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:jbound@umich.edu
2:51:3192:28:14:71:134:227:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:Sarah Turner
2:52:3204:14:15:71:177:213:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:University of Virginia
2:53:3226:15:14:71:196:198:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:Ruffner Hall, Room 294B
2:54:3249:14:14:71:158:184:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:405 Emmet Street

2:56:3295:14:14:71:123:156:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:and NBER

3:58:3324:0:14:89:92:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3: 
3:59:3324:14:28:146:467:695:12:g_d0_f4:8:g_d0_f3:Cohort Crowding:  How resources affect collegiate attainment 
3:60:3384:28:27:125:507:667:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Analyses of college enrollment and college completion rates typically focus on 
3:61:3463:27:28:89:515:640:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:demand-side factors including the financial attractiveness of a college education and the 
3:62:3553:28:28:89:483:612:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:availability of financial aid. Implicit in this line of research is the assumption that 
3:63:3641:28:27:89:513:584:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:colleges and universities adjust supply fully with changes in demand. However, there is 
3:64:3729:27:28:89:489:557:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:good reason to believe that supply is not even close to perfectly elastic. The higher 
3:65:3815:28:27:89:515:529:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:education market in the United States is dominated by public and non-profit production, 
3:66:3903:27:28:89:492:502:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and colleges and universities receive considerable subsidies from state, federal, and 
3:67:3989:28:28:89:522:474:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:private sources. Indeed, Winston (1999) estimates that student fees cover only about 12% 
3:68:4078:28:27:89:513:446:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:percent of total educational costs at public colleges and universities in the U.S.  Parallel 
3:69:4171:27:28:89:494:419:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:estimates of the average share of costs covered by student tuition and fees at private 
3:70:4258:28:27:89:520:391:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:colleges and universities are substantially higher, but still less than 50%, and much lower 
3:71:4350:27:28:89:506:364:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:at the most selective institutions. Because consumers pay only a fraction of the cost of 
3:72:4439:28:28:89:508:336:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:production, changes in demand are unlikely to be accommodated fully by colleges and 
3:73:4523:28:27:89:497:308:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:universities without commensurate increases in non-tuition revenue. For this reason, 
3:74:4608:27:28:89:498:281:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:public investment in higher education plays a crucial role in determining the degrees 
3:75:4694:28:27:89:445:253:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:produced and the supply of college-educated workers to the labor market. 
3:76:4767:27:28:125:452:226:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Significant public subsidies for college education brought about the 
3:77:4836:28:28:89:500:198:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:transformation to “mass higher education” in the post World War II decades with the 
3:78:4920:28:27:89:486:170:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:founding of community colleges and dramatic growth in public four-year colleges 
3:79:5000:27:28:89:494:143:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(Goldin, 1999).  Resources for higher education were especially plentiful in the two 
3:80:5085:28:27:89:511:115:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:decades following World War II, a period sometimes referred to as the “Golden Years” 
3:81:5170:27:0:89:506:88:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of higher education. One study sites an average annual growth rate of 8% in education 
4:82:5257:0:28:89:513:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and general expenditures per student during the decade of the 1960s  (Cheit, 1971). The 
4:83:5345:28:28:89:521:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:reach of public subsidies during this period extended to capital projects as well as current 
4:84:5438:28:27:89:518:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:expenditures, with the number of colleges rising from 1,886 institutions in 1955 to 2,573 
4:85:5528:27:28:89:517:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in 1970, a rate of almost 1 new institution per week (Finn, 1978).1  Fortunes for colleges 
4:86:5619:28:27:89:492:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:reversed dramatically at the end of the 1960s and through the 1970s, however.  The 
4:87:5702:27:28:89:470:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:aggregate link between higher education resources and collegiate attainment is 
4:88:5781:28:28:89:521:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:unmistakable: the proportion of the population completing a BA rose from about 17% for 
4:89:5868:28:27:89:520:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:those born in 1930, representing college-going in the early 1950s, to about 29% for those 
4:90:5958:27:28:89:492:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:born two decades late in 1950.  It would be hard to imagine this dramatic change in 
4:91:6042:28:27:89:523:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:attainment without a corresponding increase in public support for higher education.  After 
4:92:6133:27:28:89:502:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the 1951 birth cohort, collegiate attainment declined and then stagnated, before rising 
4:93:6221:28:28:89:273:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:again with cohorts born in the 1960s.  
4:94:6260:28:27:125:521:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The link between public funding for higher education and collegiate attainment in 
4:95:6342:27:28:89:496:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the aggregate time series provides circumstantial evidence that changes in collegiate 
4:96:6428:28:27:89:490:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:resources per student affect student outcomes. The interpretation of this time series 
4:97:6514:27:28:89:515:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:evidence is, however, open to question --  it is possible that expenditures may have been 
4:98:6604:28:28:89:511:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:responding to increases in the demand for a college education rather than the other way 
4:99:6692:28:50:89:506:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:around. This paper uses variation in the size of the college-age cohorts within states to 
4:100:6782:50:7:89:236:189:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                 
4:101:6830:7:16:125:522:182:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:1 The period from 1950 to 1970 brought about an enormous expansion in the “capital stock” of the 
4:102:6927:16:11:89:509:166:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:college and university system.  Federal and state support were integral in building the physical capital of 
4:103:7035:11:12:89:497:155:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:colleges and universities, in addition to providing subsidies for operating expenses.. For example, the 
4:104:7139:12:12:89:504:143:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:contribution from federal government sources to plant fund accumulation increased from $12.3 million 
4:105:7240:12:11:89:515:131:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:dollars in 1950 to $333.7 million in 1966, while state funds increased from $285.2 million dollars in 1950 
4:106:7347:11:12:89:515:120:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:to $738.7 million in 1966.  These data are from Table 129 of the 1972 Digest of Education Statistics, with 
4:107:7454:12:11:89:513:108:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:the Department of Education Surveys “Financial Statistics of Institutions of Higher Education” providing 
4:108:7559:11:11:89:496:97:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:the original source. In addition to the direct grants from the federal government over this interval, the 
4:109:7665:11:12:89:507:86:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:federal government also provided access to substantial (subsidized) loan funds through the Housing Act 
4:110:7768:12:0:89:135:74:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:until 1969. 
5:111:7781:0:28:89:511:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:generate plausibly exogenous variation in the availability of higher education resources 
5:112:7870:28:28:89:392:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:per student and to measure the effects on collegiate attainment 
5:113:7934:28:27:125:512:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Because only part of any observed change in current expenditures is likely to be 
5:114:8015:27:28:89:524:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:exogenous and expenditures translate to resources with long lags, direct assessment of the 
5:115:8106:28:27:89:462:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:effect of resources per student on degree outcomes using measures of current 
5:116:8183:27:28:89:520:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:expenditures or state appropriations is not appropriate. As an alternative, we focus on the 
5:117:8274:28:28:89:524:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:effects of “crowding out” brought about by relatively large cohorts within states vying for 
5:118:8366:28:27:89:506:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:a limited number of spaces in the higher education sector. Increases in the college-age 
5:119:8453:27:28:89:481:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:population shift out the higher education demand function. Yet, in the absence of 
5:120:8535:28:27:89:521:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:complete per capita adjustment in educational subsidies, increasing enrollment demand is 
5:121:8624:27:28:89:498:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:likely to reduce educational resources per student in aggregate.  When an increase in 
5:122:8710:28:28:89:520:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:cohort size is not accompanied by a proportionate increase in resources, large cohorts are 
5:123:8801:28:27:89:472:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:essentially “crowded out” of the higher education sector. To this end, plausibly 
5:124:8882:27:28:89:509:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:exogenous variation in cohort size leads to a test of how the availability of resources at 
5:125:8973:28:27:89:386:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the college and university level affects collegiate attainment.  
5:126:9038:27:28:125:512:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Within state and year, we find a strong negative association between cohort size 
5:127:9119:28:28:89:517:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and college completion rates.  Our preferred estimates suggest that a 10% increase in the 
5:128:9209:28:27:89:498:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:size of the college-age population leads to a 4% decrease in college completion rates 
5:129:9295:27:28:89:510:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:within states.   Whether the reductions in college completion rates among large cohorts 
5:130:9383:28:27:89:521:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:are caused by declines in the college-preparedness of large cohorts on the demand side of 
5:131:9473:27:28:89:513:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the higher education market or limitations in the adjustment of colleges and universities 
5:132:9563:28:0:89:523:129:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:is important to the understanding of the ‘production’ of education and the determinants of 
6:133:9655:0:28:89:506:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the supply of college-educated workers.2 We present compelling evidence that limited 
6:134:9740:28:28:89:506:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:adjustment among colleges and universities on the supply-side of the higher education 
6:135:9826:28:27:89:494:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:market is the primary cause of the relative reduction in attainment (“crowding out”) 
6:136:9911:27:28:89:199:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:among large cohorts.   
6:137:9933:28:27:125:494:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3: The first section of this analysis sets forth the defining characteristics of the 
6:138:10016:27:28:89:510:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:market for higher education in the context of a rudimentary but illustrative model.  The 
6:139:10105:28:28:89:513:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:high degree of public subsidy, the mixed market of public and non-profit providers, and 
6:140:10193:28:27:89:523:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the differentiated nature of the product in higher education lead to adjustments along both 
6:141:10285:27:28:89:517:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:quality and quantity dimensions in response to demand shocks. As a result, the predicted 
6:142:10374:28:27:89:504:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:change in undergraduate degree attainment is ultimately less than proportionate to the 
6:143:10461:27:28:89:484:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:change in cohort size, which can be thought of as a direct measure of the effect of 
6:144:10545:28:28:89:491:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:resources on collegiate attainment. The second section turns to the measurement of 
6:145:10628:28:27:89:522:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:variation within states over time in cohort size and the effect of this variable on collegiate 
6:146:10723:27:28:89:521:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:attainment. The third section focuses more directly on the nature of adjustments in higher 
6:147:10814:28:27:89:519:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:education by type of institution and the link between current revenues available to higher 
6:148:10905:27:28:89:508:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:education and cohort size. In the fourth section, we assess the empirical strength of the 
6:149:10995:28:28:89:515:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:alternative explanation that larger cohorts may have lower demand for college if college 
6:150:11084:28:27:89:510:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:preparedness (e.g., pre-collegiate achievement) is also connected to cohort size; but we 
6:151:11173:27:28:89:505:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:find such demand-side factors account for little of the reduction in college completion 
6:152:11261:28:27:89:515:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:among large cohorts. The basic finding is that the incomplete adjustment of resources in 
6:153:11350:27:28:89:509:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:response to population changes leads to a substantial reduction in collegiate attainment 
6:154:11439:28:28:89:121:129:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:rates.  
6:155:11447:28:7:89:236:101:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                 
6:156:11495:7:16:125:490:94:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:2 That within-state growth in cohort size is negatively related to collegiate attainment is an 
6:157:11590:16:0:89:511:78:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:established result in the empirical literature (Card and Lemieux (2000) also record this relationship using 
7:158:11699:0:28:125:512:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f4:The conclusion is clear: resources matter in the production of higher education.  
7:159:11780:28:28:89:511:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Changes in the funding for public colleges and universities have a large impact on both 
7:160:11868:28:27:89:493:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the quantity and the quality of college graduates in the country.  Reductions in state 
7:161:11955:27:28:89:491:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:appropriations limit the flow of resources to higher education and reduce collegiate 
7:162:12040:28:27:89:515:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:attainment, ultimately lowering the long-term supply of college-educated workers in the 
7:163:12128:27:28:89:156:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:labor market. 
7:164:12142:28:14:89:92:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3: 
7:165:12142:14:27:89:454:529:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:I. The Market for Higher Education and Responses to Demand Shocks 
7:166:12208:27:28:125:320:502:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Subsidies in the University Marketplace 
7:167:12248:28:28:125:499:474:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The substantial levels of private and public subsidies in the market for higher 
7:168:12328:28:27:89:502:446:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:education have fundamental effects on the “quantity” of collegiate attainment and the 
7:169:12414:27:28:89:518:419:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:“quality” (or resource intensity) of this product. State colleges and universities expanded 
7:170:12506:28:27:89:506:391:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:markedly in the immediate post-war years and are integral to the overall production of 
7:171:12593:27:28:89:509:364:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:college education in the United States. The share of undergraduate degrees awarded by 
7:172:12679:28:28:89:518:336:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:public institutions has increased from about 50% in 1947 to about 65% in the 2003-2004 
7:173:12766:28:27:89:509:308:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:academic year, while the share of total college enrollment (including enrollment at sub-
7:174:12854:27:28:89:446:281:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:baccalaureate institutions) is even higher at about 79% in the fall of 2004. 
7:175:12931:28:27:125:470:253:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:State colleges and universities provide the majority of higher education 
7:176:13004:27:28:89:498:226:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:enrollment opportunities to their residents at a steep discount in price, made possible 
7:177:13092:28:28:89:516:198:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:through substantial public subsidies. Table 1 summarizes the role of state appropriations 
7:178:13182:28:27:89:485:170:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in the current revenue stream of colleges and universities. Notably, state and local 
7:179:13267:27:28:89:509:143:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:support accounted for 57.5% of current revenues at the public community colleges and 
7:180:13352:28:29:89:512:115:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:more than 36% of revenues at comprehensive four-year institutions. Although the share 
7:181:13438:29:12:89:524:86:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                                                                                                                 
7:182:13582:12:0:89:190:74:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:Census and CPS data).    
8:183:13608:0:28:89:513:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of state support (30%) is somewhat lower at public research universities, the levels tend 
8:184:13698:28:28:89:499:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:to be higher, and these institutions also rely on a range of other non-tuition revenues, 
8:185:13787:28:27:89:480:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:including resources from private donations and research support from the federal 
8:186:13868:27:28:89:502:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:government. Overall, the data in this table show that tuition payments fall far short of 
8:187:13957:28:27:89:254:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:covering total educational costs.3  
8:188:13993:27:28:125:497:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:As shown in the final columns of Table 1, there are substantial differences in 
8:189:14072:28:28:89:513:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tuition prices between in-state and out-of-state tuition charges4 and between private and 
8:190:14162:28:27:89:500:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:public institutions. For this reason, public colleges and universities within a potential 
8:191:14252:27:28:89:502:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:student’s state of residence are the institutions determining the choice set for students 
8:192:14342:28:27:89:498:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:likely to be at the margin of college enrollment and completion.  Private institutions, 
8:193:14430:27:28:89:496:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:particularly the selective colleges and research universities, also rely heavily on non-
8:194:14518:28:28:89:464:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tuition sources of revenue, including donations and income from endowment. 
8:195:14593:28:27:89:501:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Nevertheless, the selective private institutions with substantial subsidies enroll a very 
8:196:14683:27:28:89:314:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:small share of the undergraduate population.5  
8:197:14730:28:27:125:503:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Non-tuition revenue affects the tradeoff between enrollment and resources per 
8:198:14808:27:28:89:476:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:student faced by colleges and universities and is critical to explaining how these 
8:199:14891:28:28:89:516:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:institutions respond to changes in the demand for higher education. We develop a model 
8:200:14978:28:27:89:503:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:that applies to a range of colleges and universities, including open-access institutions, 
8:201:15068:27:8:89:236:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                 
8:202:15116:8:16:125:489:204:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:3 Estimates by Winston mentioned earlier place the degree of subsidy even higher because 
8:203:15205:16:11:89:510:188:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:conventional statements of revenues and expenses for colleges and universities fail to account for capital 
8:204:15312:11:12:89:491:177:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:costs (both depreciation and the opportunity cost of funds), which account for an average of 25% of 
8:205:15412:12:7:89:165:165:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:educational costs.  
8:206:15432:7:16:125:489:158:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:4 For 2005-2006, the average ratio of in-state tuition to out-of-state tuition at state flagship 
8:207:15529:16:11:89:510:142:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:universities was .37, amounting to an average tuition price difference of $10,558; in states where there is

8:209:15744:12:11:89:518:119:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:charges are much larger, with differences of  $19,882 and $18,388 in Colorado and Michigan, respectively

8:211:15927:7:16:125:504:101:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:5 While the state-level is the appropriate unit of analysis for the consideration of the collegiate 
8:212:16027:16:0:89:489:85:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:attainment of students likely to be at the margin of degree attainment, the most competitive level of 
9:213:16130:0:28:89:510:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:flagship universities, and private colleges.  Variation among institutions in mission and 
9:214:16220:28:28:89:483:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:objectives, as well as resources, produce somewhat different predicted behavioral 
9:215:16302:28:27:89:259:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:adjustments by type of institution.  
9:216:16339:27:28:125:239:626:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Theoretical Motivation 
9:217:16362:28:27:125:502:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:On the supply side of the market, colleges and universities combine their own 
9:218:16440:27:28:89:516:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:resources with the energies of their students to produce collegiate attainment. University 
9:219:16531:28:28:89:519:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:revenues, representing the sum of tuition, fees, and public and private subsidies, are used 
9:220:16623:28:27:89:518:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:to purchase inputs like faculty, libraries and dormitories.   The essential choices made by 
9:221:16715:27:28:89:516:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the colleges and universities are the number of students to admit and enroll and the level 
9:222:16806:28:27:89:510:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of resources to invest per student, subject to the university’s own budget constraint and 
9:223:16896:27:28:89:511:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the level of student demand, which is a function of the quality (or resource intensity) of 
9:224:16987:28:28:89:480:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the education offered. Generating more money either through tuition revenues or 
9:225:17067:28:27:89:510:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:subsidies implies having more resources to spend on instruction.  When the non-tuition 
9:226:17154:27:28:89:507:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:share of revenues does not vary proportionately with the number of students, enrolling 
9:227:17240:28:27:89:511:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:more students necessarily implies spreading these resources more thinly.  In short, with 
9:228:17329:27:28:89:497:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the presence of non-tuition revenue, colleges and universities face a quality-quantity 
9:229:17416:28:28:89:495:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tradeoff in response to an increase in the number of students wishing to enroll at the 
9:230:17503:28:27:89:170:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:prevailing price. 
9:231:17521:27:28:125:522:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Student demand varies, as usual, with price – the higher the price, ceteris paribus, 
9:232:17606:28:27:89:496:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the lower the share of the population attending college. Demand also varies with the 
9:233:17691:27:28:89:519:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:subsidy per student (L/n, where L is the total non-tuition revenue and n is enrollment), as 
9:234:17783:28:31:89:492:129:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:students would clearly prefer to pay a given price to attend an institution with more 
9:235:17869:31:12:89:524:98:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                                                                                                                 
9:236:18013:12:12:89:502:86:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:colleges and universities (e.g., Stanford University, Harvard University) define a highly integrated and 
9:237:18118:12:0:89:506:74:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:national market that is unlikely to include students at the margin of college enrollment (Hoxby, 2000).   
10:238:18225:0:36:89:480:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:resources devoted to their education.6 We introduce the inverse demand function 
10:239:18305:36:9:140:103:673:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:),(
10:240:18308:9:0:121:139:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:pop
10:241:18311:0:17:126:132:681:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:n
10:242:18312:17:0:105:111:664:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:n
10:243:18313:0:8:105:112:681:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:L
10:244:18314:8:47:91:507:673:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f3:T, which is positive in the first argument and negative in the enrollment rate 
10:245:18393:47:9:89:229:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:among college-age students (
10:246:18421:9:0:234:252:617:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:pop
10:247:18424:0:8:239:245:634:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:n

10:249:18430:39:27:125:501:587:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Much about the behavior of colleges and universities can be inferred from the 
10:250:18508:27:28:89:470:560:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:non-distribution constraint, which applies to both public and private non-profit 
10:251:18589:28:35:89:521:532:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:institutions of higher education and requires that there be no residual shareholders.8 Most 
10:252:18681:35:10:89:382:497:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f5:generally, the nondistribution constraint is: 0)(),(=+-Lqnc
10:253:18740:10:0:337:355:487:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:pop

10:258:18761:39:28:89:505:458:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:reflects per-student costs as a function of quality (or resources), with c’(q)>0 and total 
10:259:18852:28:27:89:518:430:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:costs of n c(q). It follows that the per-student cost of the quality of education provided is 
10:260:18946:27:28:89:509:403:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:simply equal to tuition plus the per-student subsidy (L/n). Although the presentation of 
10:261:19035:28:28:89:507:375:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tuition revenue as endogenous (determined by the inverse demand function for college 
10:262:19120:28:43:89:511:347:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:enrollment) may capture the circumstances of some private institutions, it is unlikely to 
10:263:19210:43:7:89:236:304:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                 
10:264:19258:7:16:125:507:297:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:6 Comparison of tuition levels among private institutions makes this point clear. In 2005-2006, 
10:265:19354:16:12:89:514:281:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:Tufts University had tuition and required fee charges of $32,621  while Harvard University posted tuition 
10:266:19460:12:11:89:507:269:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:and fees of $32,092. With an endowment of $25.4 billion at Harvard relative to the Tufts endowment of 
10:267:19562:11:12:89:511:258:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:$845 million, it should be no surprise that demand revealed by application behavior is greater at Harvard 
10:268:19668:12:7:89:147:246:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:than at Tufts.  
10:269:19684:7:15:125:498:239:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:7 This inverse demand curve should be thought of as institution specific.  Institution-specific 
10:270:19780:15:12:89:521:224:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:demand is not infinitely elastic with respect to either tuition levels or quality because individual institutions 
10:271:19894:12:7:89:224:212:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:have considerable market power. 
10:272:19926:7:16:125:520:205:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:8 This model builds on Hansmann’s (1981) effort to illustrate the potential quality-quantity choice 
10:273:20026:16:12:89:493:189:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:for a performing arts organization when private donations depend on the quality of the presentation.  
10:274:20128:12:11:89:516:177:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:Implicitly we are assuming constant returns to scale, which we think of as a reasonable assumption for the 
10:275:20235:11:11:89:500:166:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:long run.  Also, we focus only on the choices surrounding undergraduate education, though one might 
10:276:20335:11:7:89:417:155:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:rightly consider other educational outputs such as research or graduate education. 
10:277:20418:7:17:125:508:148:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:9 In some states, a component of state funding for higher education depends on enrollment. For 
10:278:20513:17:11:89:513:131:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:simplicity, we have ignored this fact. What is important is the L rises less than proportionately with n and 
10:279:20622:11:12:89:513:120:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:this is always true. The model explains the response of students and college administrations to changes in 
10:280:20729:12:11:89:513:108:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:the size of the college-age population. Additionally, one might imagine that the cost of producing a given 
10:281:20836:11:11:89:519:97:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:level of quality, c, would depend not just on the level of quality produced, but also on enrollment rates – as 
10:282:20947:11:12:89:498:86:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:enrollment rates rise, selectivity falls. Letting c be a function of q and n/pop, rather than just q, would 
10:283:21055:12:0:89:278:74:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:complicate without changing the basic story.    
11:284:21105:0:28:89:491:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:apply to public colleges and universities where state legislators and governors have 
11:285:21190:28:28:89:339:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:considerable sway in the determination of tuition.10 
11:286:21242:28:27:125:514:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The nonlinear constraint showing the tradeoff between enrollment and resources 
11:287:21321:27:28:89:514:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:per student provides much of the intuition for analyzing the institutional adjustment to a 
11:288:21412:28:27:89:494:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:demand shock. The constraint, illustrated in Figure 1 for the case in which tuition is 
11:289:21499:27:28:89:496:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:determined exogenously, is nonlinear and asymptotes at the level of quality equal to 
11:290:21584:28:28:89:520:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tuition, as the number of students enrolled goes to infinity.11 At low levels of enrollment, 
11:291:21677:28:27:89:504:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the price of expansion is high as the relative change in per-student resource subsidy is 
11:292:21766:27:28:89:497:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:substantial. At higher levels of enrollment (and lower levels of quality), the quantity-
11:293:21854:28:27:89:524:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:quality tradeoff is less pronounced as per-student subsidy becomes a tiny piece of the cost 
11:294:21946:27:28:89:284:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of education for any individual student.  
11:295:21988:28:28:125:490:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Universities face a choice in the production of college education, implicitly 
11:296:22066:28:27:89:485:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:weighing a tradeoff between the quantity variable (or the output of the enrollment 
11:297:22149:27:28:89:509:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:decision) and the resources spent per student. How colleges and universities weigh this 
11:298:22237:28:27:89:509:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tradeoff depends on their specific “mission” (e.g., the University of Michigan has been 
11:299:22325:27:28:89:517:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:given different objectives by the state than Central Michigan University) and their initial 
11:300:22417:28:28:89:522:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:levels of endowment or fixed subsidy. We assume that institutions maximize an objective 
11:301:22505:28:27:89:507:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:function that includes both quantity (n) and quality (q) components – U(n,q) subject to 
11:302:22593:27:36:89:260:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the constraint 0)(=+-LqncnT.  
11:303:22623:36:7:89:236:176:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                 
11:304:22671:7:16:125:493:169:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:10 A survey of State Higher Education Executive Officers finds that legislatures in 10 states 
11:305:22765:16:12:89:491:153:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:explicitly set tuition in practice or in statute (Christal, 1997). In other states, tuition determination is 
11:306:22874:12:11:89:520:141:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:generally the responsibility of governing boards or state higher education authorities, with these authorities 
11:307:22985:11:7:89:387:130:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:often composed of political appointees (Kane, Orszag, and Gunter, 2003). 
11:308:23058:7:16:125:519:123:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:11 The basic “shape” of the constraint would be the same with the endogenous treatment of tuition 
11:309:23156:16:21:89:518:107:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:though the slope would be steeper and the function would “flatten” at a greater rate. With tuition fixed, the 
11:310:23266:21:0:89:416:86:10:g_d0_f3:7:g_d0_f3:slope of the constraint is 2/nL-, while endogenous tuition yields 2212

11:314:23341:7:10:465:472:86:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:T

11:317:23346:10:0:407:413:76:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:n
11:318:23347:0:17:413:420:93:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:L
