11:320:23349:0:7:380:375:93:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:qc
11:321:23351:7:0:478:441:86:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:-+
11:322:23353:0:7:404:411:93:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:-
11:323:23354:7:10:394:401:86:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:=
11:324:23355:10:0:371:377:76:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:∂
11:325:23356:0:7:364:370:93:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:∂
11:326:23357:7:0:502:507:86:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:. 
12:327:23361:0:28:125:499:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:There is no reason to believe that the objective function is the same across all 
12:328:23442:28:28:89:492:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:universities or that all universities and colleges have access to generous non-tuition 
12:329:23529:28:27:89:517:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:revenues. Some institutional leaders may have ambitions to upgrade quality while others 
12:330:23617:27:28:89:489:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:may find rewards (or requirements from the state) in maximizing enrollment. How 
12:331:23697:28:27:89:519:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:quality and enrollment adjust to changes in demand, driven by population variation or by 
12:332:23786:27:28:89:499:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:changes in subsidy, brought about by state fiscal shocks will depend on the objective 
12:333:23872:28:28:89:521:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:function governing the behavior of the university and the level of subsidy available to the 
12:334:23964:28:27:89:516:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:university. At one extreme, potentially representative of the public research universities, 
12:335:24056:27:28:89:487:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:institutions weigh the tradeoff between quantity and quality directly and choose to 
12:336:24140:28:27:89:453:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:operate at the tangency between the institutional indifference curve and the 
12:337:24217:27:28:89:505:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:nondistribution constraint (illustrated in the top panel of Figure 1). In such a situation, 
12:338:24309:28:28:89:508:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:enrollment may be independent of student demand (with the demand function crossing 
12:339:24392:28:27:89:494:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the constraint at or to the right of the tangency). Shifts in demand do not change the 
12:340:24479:27:28:89:510:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:optimal enrollment-quality choice of the institution, but rather lead to or exacerbate the 
12:341:24570:28:27:89:232:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:condition of excess demand.  
12:342:24599:27:28:125:523:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:In the alternative case where institutions simply maximize enrollment, the level of 
12:343:24683:28:28:89:518:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:enrollment (and the quality of the collegiate experience) is determined at the intersection 
12:344:24775:28:27:89:475:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of the demand function and the constraint in quality-enrollment space.  Without 
12:345:24855:27:28:89:512:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:compensating increases in subsidy (L), which would shift out the constraint, the shift in 
12:346:24945:28:27:89:504:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:demand occurs along a function with declining resources per student, leading to a less 
12:347:25032:27:28:89:517:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:than proportionate increase in enrollment demand. This is illustrated in Figure 1 (bottom 
12:348:25122:28:28:89:514:129:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:panel); along the downward sloping part of the constraint, the change in enrollment will 
12:349:25211:28:0:89:521:101:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:be less than the shift in demand. What is more, quality (resources per student) declines as 
13:350:25305:0:28:89:515:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:quantity (enrollment) increases along the constraint. Note that, in addition to the decline 
13:351:25397:28:28:89:479:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in enrollment, this decline in resources per student will have a negative effect on 
13:352:25481:28:27:89:486:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:collegiate attainment so long as resources are an input in the collegiate production 
13:353:25566:27:28:89:134:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:process.  
13:354:25576:28:27:125:518:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The implication of the model is clear: unless resources increase to match changes 
13:355:25658:27:28:89:492:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in demand, increases in cohort size are likely to lead to lower proportional levels of 
13:356:25745:28:28:89:517:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:college enrollment and completion. Increases in cohort size will lead to reductions in the 
13:357:25836:28:27:89:472:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:share of the cohort receiving BA (undergraduate) degrees because colleges and 
13:358:25914:27:28:89:500:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:universities must trade resources per student, which include substantial subsidies, for 
13:359:26002:28:27:89:500:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:larger enrollments. The extent to which institutions respond to changes in cohort size 
13:360:26089:27:28:89:492:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:with adjustments in resources per student, tuition price, and enrollment will depend 
13:361:26174:28:28:89:515:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:significantly on the mission and control of the college and university. In this section, we 
13:362:26266:28:27:89:516:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:highlight the circumstances of public institutions because they enroll the largest share of 
13:363:26358:27:28:89:512:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:higher education students and are central to the market; yet, for reasons given, the basic 
13:364:26449:28:27:89:429:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:insights apply more broadly to all non-profit colleges and universities. 
13:365:26522:27:28:125:496:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:While the magnitude of the response to an increase in cohort size in terms of 
13:366:26600:28:36:89:468:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:collegiate output – measured by degrees awarded – will vary with institutional 
13:367:26679:36:9:89:310:231:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:circumstance, the model predicts 1
13:368:26713:9:0:261:270:222:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ln
13:369:26715:0:8:263:272:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ln
13:370:26717:8:9:296:303:231:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:<
13:371:26718:9:0:253:259:222:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:∂
13:372:26719:0:17:256:262:239:12:g_d0_f6:12:g_d0_f6:∂
13:373:26720:17:0:272:291:222:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Pop
13:374:26723:0:8:275:290:239:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:BA
13:375:26725:8:38:311:501:231:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:at the state level in the presence of non-
13:376:26767:38:28:89:510:193:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tuition revenues that are invariant to cohort size. The strength of this effect will depend 
13:377:26859:28:28:89:516:165:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:on where the institution begins on the budget constraint (e.g., on the steep portion where 
13:378:26950:28:27:89:519:137:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the quantity / quality tradeoff is high) and how resources contribute to the transformation 
13:379:27042:27:28:89:264:110:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of enrollment to degree attainment.  
13:380:27079:28:0:89:95:82:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:  
14:381:27083:0:27:89:364:708:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:II. Cohort Size and College Completion within States 
14:382:27135:27:28:125:518:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Our empirical approach begins with the measurement of the relationship between 
14:383:27214:28:27:89:499:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:cohort size and college completion within states.  States are a natural unit of analysis 
14:384:27303:27:28:89:493:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:because public institutions of higher education are largely under the control of state 
14:385:27390:28:27:89:499:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:governments.  By looking at changes within states over time, we abstract from broad 
14:386:27474:27:28:89:509:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:national changes.  While aggregate adjustments in cohort size may affect the returns to 
14:387:27562:28:28:89:510:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:college, within-state variation in cohort size should not affect returns in the presence of 
14:388:27654:28:27:89:277:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:nationally integrated labor markets.12   
14:389:27695:27:28:125:522:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The empirical analysis in this section focuses on the measurement of the elasticity 
14:390:27779:28:27:89:514:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of college completion, defined as the log of BA degrees awarded, with respect to cohort 
14:391:27867:27:28:89:504:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:size. The state is the unit of analysis and all specifications include state and year fixed 
14:392:27959:28:28:89:505:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:effects.   Thus, regression estimates of collegiate attainment of a cohort on cohort size 
14:393:28049:28:27:89:513:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:examine whether the collegiate outcome of a cohort differs when that state and cohort’s 
14:394:28137:27:28:89:393:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:population changes at a rate different from the national norm.   
14:395:28202:28:27:125:497:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Given our interest in how cohort size affects the resources available to those  
14:396:28282:27:28:89:487:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:considering college attendance, it is natural to measure cohort size at a point when 
14:397:28367:28:28:89:498:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:individuals would typically attend college (age 18). We would then like to be able to 
14:398:28453:28:27:89:514:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:calculate the fraction of these cohorts that eventually receive a BA degree. Such data do 
14:399:28543:27:28:89:519:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:not exist.  Measures are available from colleges and universities enumerating the number 
14:400:28632:28:7:89:236:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                 
14:401:28680:7:16:125:520:177:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:12 Larger cohorts may experience somewhat lower returns to education if the substitution between 
14:402:28777:16:12:89:511:161:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:younger and older workers among the college educated is less than complete, thus reducing incentives to 
14:403:28881:12:11:89:513:149:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:complete college in aggregate. A long research literature (e.g., Welch, 1979; Stapleton and Young, 1988) 
14:404:28986:11:12:89:518:138:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:focuses on the question of how adjustments of the labor market to relatively large cohorts affect incentives 
14:405:29095:12:11:89:513:126:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:to invest in education for relatively large cohorts.  Using Census data, we find no evidence of variation in 
14:406:29204:11:12:89:496:115:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:state-specific college/high school wage differentials with cohort size. In addition, while state specific 
14:407:29310:12:11:89:512:103:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:cohort size is (negatively) associated with state specific unemployment rates, we also find no evidence of 
14:408:29417:11:0:89:510:92:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:any association between unemployment and college completion rates (see also Card and Lemieux, 2000; 
15:409:29519:0:28:89:519:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of degrees conferred each year (we refer to this as “institutional data”). Alternatively, the 
15:410:29613:28:28:89:517:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:decennial Census allows researchers to calculate degree completion by state of birth, but 
15:411:29703:28:27:89:506:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:does not identify state of residence at age 18.   Using both sources of data allows us to 
15:412:29793:27:28:89:520:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:check the potential effect of the differences between the two data sets on our estimates of 
15:413:29885:28:27:89:147:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f4:crowding.   
15:414:29896:27:28:125:520:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:We begin with the presentation of results based on institutional data and then turn 
15:415:29980:28:28:89:489:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:to results employing Census data. Finally, we discuss possible explanations for the 
15:416:30064:28:27:89:483:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:differences between the two sets of results.  In the main, results using Census and 
15:417:30148:27:28:89:525:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:institutional data tell a very similar story: increases in cohort size are not accompanied by  
15:418:30243:28:27:89:334:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:commensurate increases in collegiate attainment.   
15:419:30294:27:28:125:128:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3: 
15:420:30294:28:28:89:266:405:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Results Based on Institutional Data  
15:421:30331:28:27:125:491:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Table 2 begins the empirical analysis with the estimation of the elasticity of 
15:422:30410:27:28:89:501:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:college completion, defined as the log of BA degrees awarded, with respect to cohort 
15:423:30495:28:27:89:497:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:size, defined as the size of the population age 18 within the state four years earlier.13 
15:424:30584:27:28:89:519:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Estimates are presented for two periods: 1954-1996 and 1967-1996. Columns (1) and (2) 
15:425:30670:28:28:89:522:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:show that the elasticity of college completion at all institutions with respect to cohort size 
15:426:30765:28:27:89:478:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:is significantly less than one, varying from .71 to .62 depending on the period of 
15:427:30848:27:28:89:521:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:observation.  In addition, the estimate in column (3) for the effect of cohort size at age 18 
15:428:30942:28:52:89:522:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:on the number of degrees awarded by public institutions is broadly similar to the estimate 
15:429:31033:52:12:89:524:132:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                                                                                                                 
15:430:31177:12:12:89:519:120:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:Fitzpatrick and Turner, 2006).  These estimates suggest that the primary effect of variation in state-specific 
15:431:31288:12:7:89:533:108:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f4:college-age population size on college completion rates cannot be working through labor market incentives.     
15:432:31398:7:15:125:522:101:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:13  Thus, for example, we related BA’s produced in Michigan in 1990 to the size of the 18 year old 
15:433:31497:15:12:89:497:86:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:population in the state in 1986.  The choice of this four year lag was dictated by the fact that 22 is the 
15:434:31604:12:0:89:335:74:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:modal age at which individuals receive their BA in the U.S.   
16:435:31668:0:28:89:544:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of overall effect (.59 vs. .62) over the period for which this distinction is possible.14                  
16:436:31775:28:28:89:507:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Another variable of interest in measuring higher educational attainment is enrollment.  
16:437:31863:28:27:89:519:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Enrollment represents an “input” to educational attainment while degrees awarded are an 
16:438:31951:27:28:89:489:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:outcome. For this reason, we regard degrees received rather than enrollment as our 
16:439:32034:28:27:89:523:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:primary outcome and would expect effects of cohort size on enrollment to be more muted 
16:440:32121:27:28:89:497:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:than the effects on degree completion. Using measures of total enrollment presents a 
16:441:32206:28:28:89:515:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:story similar to the regressions for degree outcomes.  With the log of total enrollment as 
16:442:32297:28:27:89:496:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the outcome in columns (4)-(6) of Table 2, estimates of the elasticity with respect to 
16:443:32384:27:28:89:519:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:cohort size are larger for enrollment (0.89 and 0.79), while still appreciably less than one 
16:444:32477:28:27:89:222:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in all of the specifications.  
16:445:32508:27:28:125:497:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Figure 2 plots per-year cohort size and BA production from 1950 to 1995 for 
16:446:32584:28:28:89:506:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:selected states. (Similar figures for all states appear in the appendix). It is important to 
16:447:32677:28:27:89:514:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:note that while the regressions we reported in Table 2 took out year effects, much of the 
16:448:32767:27:28:89:523:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:observed variation in these figures represents the growth and decline of the overall size of 
16:449:32860:28:27:89:505:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the college-aged population.  For each of the states shown, the 1950s and 1960s saw a 
16:450:32946:27:28:89:485:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:very dramatic rise in both cohort size and the number of individuals receiving BA 
16:451:33028:28:28:89:514:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:degrees. However, around 1970 BA production leveled off and remained approximately 
16:452:33111:28:27:89:493:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:constant in the subsequent 25 years, despite an expansion in cohort size through the 
16:453:33196:27:28:89:495:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:1980s and a drop in cohort size thereafter. The figures give the strong impression of 
16:454:33282:28:27:89:502:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:capacity constraints and the absence of full elasticity in supply. During the 1950s and 
16:455:33370:27:37:89:493:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:1960s many states were investing heavily in higher education. As a result, capacity, 
16:456:33455:37:7:89:236:120:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                 
16:457:33503:7:16:125:503:113:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:14 Following Fortin (2003), one might be concerned that the market adjustments in states with 
16:458:33597:16:11:89:512:97:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:smaller private higher education sectors would be different from those in states with larger private higher 
16:459:33705:11:12:89:481:86:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:education sectors. Interacting cohort size with an indicator for states with public higher education 
16:460:33806:12:0:89:349:74:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:enrollment below the median does not yield a significant effect.  
17:461:33874:0:28:89:502:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:enrollments, and BA production expanded rapidly. Starting in the 1970s, investments 
17:462:33958:28:28:89:417:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:leveled off and capacity and enrollments remained relatively fixed.  
17:463:34027:28:27:125:506:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The pattern observed in Figure 2 suggests that, while BA production might not 
17:464:34105:27:28:89:501:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:have dropped much during periods when cohort size was shrinking, it would increase 
17:465:34188:28:27:89:493:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:during periods when cohort size was increasing. This kind of asymmetric pattern of 
17:466:34271:27:28:89:485:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:adjustment might arise if it were politically harder to shrink than to expand higher 
17:467:34356:28:28:89:496:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:education. To check on this notion, we re-estimated our regression models using the 
17:468:34440:28:27:89:516:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:period before and after 1970. The point estimates suggest that, if anything, crowding out 
17:469:34530:27:28:89:502:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(i.e., the less-than-proportional increase in BA production relative to cohort size) was 
17:470:34619:28:27:89:502:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:more severe before 1970s than it was after, though the difference was not statistically 
17:471:34707:27:28:89:489:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:significant.  More generally, our results do not depend on the period or set of years 
17:472:34793:28:28:89:193:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:chosen for analysis.   
17:473:34816:28:27:125:473:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Still, there are a number of reasons why the institutional measure of BA 
17:474:34889:27:28:89:515:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:production regressed on cohort size at age 18 might tend to exaggerate the magnitude of 
17:475:34977:28:27:89:516:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:cohort crowding. It would not be too surprising to find that being born into a large (state-
17:476:35069:27:28:89:513:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:specific) cohort would tend to increase the odds that a person went out of state to attend 
17:477:35160:28:28:89:513:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:college or temporarily postponed going to college. In either case our estimates based on 
17:478:35249:28:27:89:488:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the institutional data, would overstate the magnitude of the effect of cohort size on 
17:479:35335:27:28:89:503:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:college completion (crowding). The Residence and Migration survey, which has been 
17:480:35417:28:27:89:511:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:conducted periodically by the Department of Education since the 1940s, provides some 
17:481:35502:27:28:89:521:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:information on enrollment by state of residence and college. We estimate the elasticity of 
17:482:35593:28:28:89:479:129:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:total enrollment at the state level with respect to population [0.61 (0.11)] and the 
17:483:35678:28:0:89:493:101:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:elasticity of enrollment of state residents with respect to population [0.62 (0.08)]. If 
18:484:35769:0:28:89:483:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:increased out-of-state enrollment were a substantial part of the response to cohort 
18:485:35853:28:28:89:513:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:expansion, we would see larger elasticities (closer to 1) for enrollment of state residents 
18:486:35945:28:27:89:492:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:than for total enrollment in the state. Yet, the elasticities for these two measures are 
18:487:36034:27:28:89:497:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:virtually identical. The available empirical evidence does not support the notion that 
18:488:36121:28:27:89:516:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:relatively large cohorts of college students are “absorbed” by neighboring states. In fact, 
18:489:36213:27:28:89:518:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:it appears that the enrollment behavior of state residents is closely tied to in-state college 
18:490:36308:28:28:89:494:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:opportunities, with elasticities very close to the estimates using the full panel of BA 
18:491:36396:28:27:89:213:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:degree data in Table 2.15  
18:492:36423:27:28:125:502:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:In addition, it seems plausible that time to degree may tend to rise with cohort 
18:493:36504:28:27:89:491:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:size. If college-age individuals in large cohorts face fewer resources, they may find 
18:494:36590:27:28:89:511:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:college less attractive, and marginal students might delay college enrollment.16 What is 
18:495:36679:28:28:89:515:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:more, fewer resources might translate into slower progress while in school. Empirically, 
18:496:36768:28:27:89:520:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:calculations using the Current Population Survey suggest that increasing cohort size does 
18:497:36858:27:28:89:509:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:increase the age at degree receipt17, though the magnitude of this effect is by no means 
18:498:36947:28:27:89:519:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:large enough to explain the overall effect on completion rates. One concern is that, in the 
18:499:37039:27:28:89:513:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:institutional measures of degree attainment, increases in time to degree will tend to bias 
18:500:37130:28:28:89:499:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:estimates of the effect of cohort size on the number of BAs produced per cohort; yet, 
18:501:37216:28:38:89:495:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:such errors should be offsetting as time to degree expands and contracts with cohort 
18:502:37301:38:7:89:236:201:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                 
18:503:37349:7:17:125:523:194:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:15 Focusing on just the flagships schools, we do find that out-of-state enrollment tends to fall in the 
18:504:37453:17:11:89:521:177:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:face of large cohorts; however this effect is small enough and the flagship schools represent a small enough 
18:505:37562:11:11:89:496:166:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:share of total enrollment, that this pattern hardly shows up when we combine data on all colleges and 
18:506:37664:11:7:89:142:155:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f4:universities.  
18:507:37678:7:17:125:488:148:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:16 If shifts in population size occurred at fairly high frequency, individuals who are part of 
18:508:37773:17:11:89:519:131:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:particularly large cohorts might find it optimal to postpone college for several years. However, this kind of 
18:509:37883:11:12:89:507:120:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:substitution is unlikely to be plausible in large scale as, as we will see, most of the movements in cohort 
18:510:37991:12:11:89:507:108:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:size observed occur at relatively low frequency. Moreover, such inter-temporal adjustment would likely 
18:511:38094:11:11:89:506:97:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:reduce the number of years over which an individual would get the benefits of a college education, thus 
18:512:38198:11:7:89:227:86:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:reducing the return to the degree.  
18:513:38234:7:0:125:429:79:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:17 Falaris and Peters (1992) find similar patterns using NLS and PSID data. 
19:514:38312:0:28:89:512:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:size.18 All in all, we doubt these inter-temporal substitution effects have much effect on 
19:515:38403:28:28:89:165:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:our estimates.   
19:516:38420:28:27:125:477:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:We should note that with the census data we can directly measure cohort 
19:517:38492:27:28:89:508:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:completion rates.  As a result, effects of cohort size on the location or timing of degree 
19:518:38583:28:27:89:492:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:completion will not affect our estimates of the relationship between cohort size and 
19:519:38668:27:28:89:190:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:degree completion.   
19:520:38689:28:28:89:239:543:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Results Based on Census Data 
19:521:38718:28:27:125:498:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:An alternative way to measure the effect of cohort size on degree completion 
19:522:38795:27:28:89:521:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:employs Census micro data on BA completion rates, organized by state of birth withbirth 
19:523:38883:28:27:89:516:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:cohort size as the relevant demographic variable.19 Using alternative data sources serves 
19:524:38973:27:28:89:480:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:to underscore the reliability of our results while necessarily answering somewhat 
19:525:39055:28:28:89:513:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:different questions. Table 3 presents estimates of the effects of cohort size (measured in 
19:526:39146:28:27:89:497:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:terms of year and state of birth) on degree completion using Census data, along with 
19:527:39231:27:8:89:236:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                 
19:528:39279:8:16:125:522:342:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:18 A simple example will illustrate. Suppose that the fraction of a cohort ultimately receiving a BA 
19:529:39380:16:11:89:492:326:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:remains constant, but cohort size grows at a rate of 1% per year. Also suppose that, as a result of the 
19:530:39484:11:12:89:505:315:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:increase in cohort size, the age at which individuals typically receive their BA rises from 22 to 23 years 
19:531:39591:12:11:89:520:303:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:over a 10-year period. During this period we would observe an approximate 10% increase in the size of the 
19:532:39697:11:12:89:514:292:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:population, but only a 9% increase in the size of the number of BAs. As a result, we would underestimate 
19:533:39802:12:11:89:513:280:10:g_d0_f8:10:g_d0_f3:-ln(BA) / - ln(Pop) by about 10% – or estimate crowd out when none exists. This example might make it 
19:534:39904:11:12:89:509:269:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:seem as if the data we are using will lead us to overestimate crowd out effects. However, if one runs this 
19:535:40011:12:11:89:517:257:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:thought experiment in reverse, one sees that during periods of declining cohort size (and decreased time to 
19:536:40119:11:12:89:518:246:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:degree), we will tend to overestimate -ln(BA) / - ln(Pop). Since the variables we use in our regressions are 
19:537:40228:12:11:89:519:234:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:all deviations from year and state means, some states will be experiencing increases in cohort size (relative 
19:538:40338:11:12:89:522:223:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:to the mean), while others are experiencing decreases in cohort size, and the bias introduced by the fact that 
19:539:40449:12:7:89:380:211:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:we are using period rather than cohort measures will tend to cancel out.  
19:540:40523:7:16:125:509:204:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:19 Note that when we use Census micro data organized by state and year of birth to measure the 
19:541:40618:16:11:89:507:188:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:college completion rates, the regression estimates with the dependent variable specified as a completion 
19:542:40723:11:12:89:509:177:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:rate (ln[BA/N]) are not equal to the specification in levels (ln[BA]) minus one. The reason is that that the 
19:543:40832:12:11:89:523:165:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:denominator in the measure of the completion rate, a measure of cohort size calculated from the micro data, 
19:544:40940:11:12:89:507:154:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:is not identical to the population measure from Vital Statistics sources. What we observe in the data is a 
19:545:41047:12:11:89:518:142:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:negative correlation between the error (the difference in the measures) and the measure of population from 
19:546:41154:11:12:89:503:131:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:the Vital Statistics source. While the simple correlation between the population measure from the Vital 
19:547:41258:12:11:89:497:119:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:Statistics and the population measure from the Census micro data is 0.996, a regression of the Census 
19:548:41360:11:12:89:523:108:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:measure on the Vital Statistics measure (both measured in logs), produces a coefficient of .952 (.0127). The 
19:549:41469:12:0:89:520:96:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:result is that regression estimates using the Census micro data in levels will produce estimates suggesting a 
20:550:41581:0:28:89:515:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:estimates of the effects on degrees conferred using the institutional data for comparison. 
20:551:41672:28:28:89:485:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Because the dependent variable for the Census-based estimates is the cohort share 
20:552:41754:28:27:89:498:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(fraction with a BA) while for the institutional-based results it is the number of BAs, 
20:553:41842:27:28:89:509:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:subtracting one from the latter results will put these two estimates on the same footing. 
20:554:41932:28:27:89:512:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Regressions of the share of the population born in each state and year with a BA degree 
20:555:42020:27:28:89:523:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:on cohort size (this time measured with cohort size at the year of birth) show a substantial 
20:556:42113:28:28:89:509:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:decline in college completion shares with changes in cohort size. The magnitude of the 
20:557:42200:28:27:89:521:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:estimated decline when using the Census data is somewhat smaller than the magnitude of 
20:558:42287:27:28:89:521:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the decline when using the institutional data – that is -0.26 or -0.24 versus -0.34 or –0.29, 
20:559:42381:28:27:89:505:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:but the differences between the point estimates are not qualitatively important, nor are 
20:560:42470:27:28:89:239:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:they statistically significant.20  
20:561:42505:28:28:125:523:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:To address the potential concern that the identification of the effect of within-state 
20:562:42592:28:27:89:519:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:changes in cohort size on degree attainment is entirely a reflection of broad demographic 
20:563:42682:27:28:89:486:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:shifts, we add state-specific linear trends to the baseline specification.  Results are 
20:564:42770:28:27:89:521:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:reported in the even-numbered columns of Table 3.  While including state-specific trends 
20:565:42859:27:28:89:519:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in the specifications using Census data tends to weaken the evidence for “crowding out,” 
20:566:42948:28:28:89:491:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:doing so in the specifications using institutional data strengthens it, though perhaps 
20:567:43035:28:27:89:519:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:implausibly (even columns, bottom row of Table 3).   Understanding the large difference 
20:568:43123:27:28:89:518:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:between the results based on the institutional data and the Census data with the inclusion 
20:569:43214:28:27:89:492:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of state-specific trends would certainly seem to be important. Possible explanations 
20:570:43299:27:12:89:524:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                                                                                                                 
20:571:43443:12:12:89:520:145:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:lower elasticity of completion with respect to population than related estimates with the dependent variable 
20:572:43552:12:7:89:218:133:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:specified as a completion rate.   
20:573:43586:7:16:125:520:126:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:20 These estimates are quite similar to those presented in Card and Lemieux (2000). Depending on 
20:574:43683:16:11:89:492:110:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:the specification and cohorts used in their regressions, they estimate a 10% increase in cohort size is 
20:575:43787:11:12:89:497:99:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:associated with a decline in college completion of between 0.4 and 0.8 percentage points for men and 
20:576:43888:12:0:89:522:87:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:between 0.3 and 0.6 percentage points for women.  To convert these estimates into elasticities, one needs to 
21:577:43999:0:28:89:497:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:include differences between the two in the timing of the cohort-size variable and the 
21:578:44084:28:28:89:516:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:potential for significant measurement error in the state-specific measure of population at 
21:579:44175:28:27:89:130:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:age 18.  
21:580:44184:27:28:125:492:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Given our maintained hypothesis that the primary reason for the association 
21:581:44260:28:27:89:507:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:between cohort size and college completion rates involves cohort crowding at colleges 
21:582:44346:27:28:89:497:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and universities, the right measure of cohort size is the cohort size during the typical 
21:583:44435:28:28:89:513:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:college going years. Cohort size at birth can then be thought of as an error-ridden proxy 
21:584:44525:28:27:89:488:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:for cohort size 18 years later. Under the assumption that the variable we wanted to 
21:585:44609:27:28:89:516:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:include in these regression was the size of the population in a person’s state of residence 
21:586:44701:28:27:89:523:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:when the person was in high school, we use Census data to calculate the magnitude of the 
21:587:44790:27:28:89:509:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:attenuation bias associated with using the size of the population in the person’s state of 
21:588:44881:28:28:89:507:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:residence at birth.  To do this we use data on 15-17 year olds in the 1960-1990 Census 
21:589:44968:28:27:89:504:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and regress the log of current state-specific cohort size on the log of the person’s state 
21:590:45059:27:28:89:505:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:specific birth cohort size, controlling for state and year effects.  The coefficient on the 
21:591:45151:28:27:89:498:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:birth cohort variable in these regressions is 0.56 (0.10), implying a downward bias if 
21:592:45238:27:28:89:507:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:44%.  Once state specific trends are included in the regression, the coefficient drops to 
21:593:45328:28:28:89:500:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:0.30 (0.06), implying a downward bias of 70%.  We use these estimates to adjust our 
21:594:45412:28:27:89:520:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Census-based estimates, with corrected estimates reported in the second row of Table 3.   
21:595:45502:27:28:89:506:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:These corrected results are remarkably consistent across specifications and years – the 
21:596:45590:28:27:89:475:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:estimates imply that a 10% increase in state specific cohort size reduces college 
21:597:45672:27:59:89:518:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:completion rates by about 4%.   The corrected Census based estimates are also similar in 
21:598:45761:59:12:89:524:98:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                                                                                                                 
21:599:45905:12:12:89:498:86:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:divide by the average fraction receiving a BA in these cohorts – somewhat more than 0.2 for men and 
21:600:46005:12:0:89:238:74:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:somewhat less than 0.2 for women.   
22:601:46043:0:28:89:508:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:magnitude to the estimates based on institutional data that do not include state-specific 
22:602:46133:28:28:89:130:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:trends.   
22:603:46143:28:27:125:520:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Because including state-specific trends eliminates more than 80% of the variation 
22:604:46225:27:28:89:506:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:in our key explanatory variable, one natural hypothesis is that the introduction of state-
22:605:46315:28:27:89:513:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:specific trends seriously exacerbates the impact of measurement error in our cohort size 
22:606:46404:27:28:89:505:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:measures on outcomes. The intercensal estimates of cohort size at age 18 are based on 
22:607:46490:28:28:89:510:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:imputations that, importantly, involve Census estimates of migration flows, which may 
22:608:46576:28:27:89:518:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:reflect divergence from the actual level of the population within a state in a given year.21 
22:609:46668:27:28:89:483:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Gauging the direct effect of these errors on our estimates would be difficult if not 
22:610:46753:28:27:89:522:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:impossible.   Notably, the Vital Statistic data that we use to construct the birth cohort size 
22:611:46848:27:28:89:490:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:variables used in our analysis of Census data does not suffer from the same kind of 
22:612:46932:28:28:89:356:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:measurement error problems generated by imputation.  
22:613:46985:28:27:125:505:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Regressions that use institutional measures of degree attainment (without state-
22:614:47065:27:28:89:515:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:specific trends) produce estimates of the effect of cohort size on college completion that 
22:615:47156:28:27:89:520:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:are close to the ones we obtain using Census data adjusted for the imperfect link between 
22:616:47246:27:28:89:478:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:cohort size at birth and the size of cohorts at the time of college entry.  With this 
22:617:47332:28:28:89:492:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:adjustment, Census-based estimates produce nearly the same estimated elasticity of 
22:618:47415:28:27:89:493:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:college completion with and without state-specific trends, and these point estimates 
22:619:47500:27:28:89:525:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:actually suggest slightly more crowding than those estimates relying on institutional data.  
22:620:47593:28:27:89:490:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Overall, these results are consistent with the interpretation that the “crowding” that 
22:621:47680:27:28:89:270:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:matters occurs at the collegiate level. 
22:622:47720:28:7:89:236:129:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                 
22:623:47768:7:16:125:522:122:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:21 Some evidence on the magnitude of the errors introduced by the census imputations comes from 
22:624:47864:16:12:89:490:106:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:comparing estimates based on imputations to estimates based on actual enumerations. Comparisons 
22:625:47960:12:0:89:511:94:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:between Census projections of the college-age population in each state in 2000 benchmarked to the 1990 
23:626:48065:0:28:89:369:709:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Understanding the Variation in our Population Measures 
23:627:48120:28:28:125:484:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Geographic shifts in the location of economic activity in the U.S. generate 
23:628:48196:28:27:89:513:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:migration across states, with regions in the South and West growing much more rapidly 
23:629:48282:27:28:89:518:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:than those in the North East and North Central, and generate much of the variation in the 
23:630:48372:28:27:89:511:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:cohort size measures. Geographic mobility of adults of childbearing age and those with 
23:631:48459:27:28:89:487:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:children generates changes in the size of the pool of potential college-age students 
23:632:48544:28:28:89:515:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:within-states some years later. Indeed, the within-state-and-year correlation between the 
23:633:48634:28:27:89:510:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:size of the 18-year old population and employment (both measured in logs) is 0.72 (the 
23:634:48721:27:28:89:451:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:correlation between the size of the birth cohort and employment is 0.67).    
23:635:48798:28:27:125:517:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:To get a perspective on the time series properties or our population measures, we 
23:636:48880:27:28:89:515:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:employ Fourier decomposition following Baker, Benjamin, and Stanger (1999), and we  
23:637:48964:28:28:89:495:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:find the vast majority of the total variation in the data in the low frequency range.22  
23:638:49053:28:27:89:496:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Indeed, with either population variable, over two-thirds of the variance occurs at the 
23:639:49140:27:28:89:517:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:lowest two frequencies identified in the data (frequencies corresponding to periods of 44 
23:640:49230:28:27:89:516:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:and 22 years), before state specific trends are taken out.  Even with the inclusion of state 
23:641:49323:27:28:89:485:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:specific trends, three-fifths of the variation occurs as the lowest two frequencies.   
23:642:49410:28:28:125:514:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:State-specific shifts in the size of the college-age population tend to be slow and 
23:643:49494:28:27:89:506:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:steady and are, as a result, highly predictable. The predictable nature of changes in the 
23:644:49584:27:28:89:508:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:size of the college-age population within states and the observation that growing states 
23:645:49673:28:27:89:523:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:may experience less budgetary pressure would, at the surface, mitigate against findings of 
23:646:49764:27:12:89:524:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                                                                                                                 
23:647:49908:12:12:89:511:145:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:enumeration to counts from the 2000 enumeration show an average gap of roughly 4%, with some errors 
23:648:50008:12:7:89:182:133:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:being as large as 20%! 
23:649:50031:7:16:125:524:126:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:22  With 43 years of data (1954-1996), we employ the Fourier decomposition to divide the measure 
23:650:50128:16:0:89:478:110:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:of cohort size into 22 orthogonal components at varying frequencies (from 0 to -) according to:  
24:651:50227:0:28:89:504:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:substantial crowding out at the postsecondary level.  That such effects exist, given the 
24:652:50316:28:28:89:486:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:nature of the variation in cohort size, point to the importance of substantial capital 
24:653:50403:28:27:89:513:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:subsidies in higher education and the limited degree of supply-side adjustments at those 
24:654:50492:27:28:89:271:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:institutions like research universities. 
24:655:50533:28:28:89:92:598:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4: 
24:656:50533:28:27:89:370:570:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:III. Cohort Size and Collegiate Supply-Side Responses 
24:657:50587:27:28:125:481:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:To understand the mechanism behind the limited adjustment of collegiate 
24:658:50659:28:27:89:466:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:attainment with increases in cohort size, we consider the potential supply-side 
24:659:50739:27:28:89:510:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:adjustments of colleges and universities.  Potential margins of adjustment include price 
24:660:50828:28:27:89:521:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(tuition) and resources per student, with changes in these avenues potentially differing by 
24:661:50920:27:28:89:183:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:type of institution.  
24:662:50942:28:28:125:223:405:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Tuition adjustments 
24:663:50962:28:27:125:520:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:For public institutions, tuition covers such a small fraction of total costs and there 
24:664:51049:27:28:89:515:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:is such an unusual link between political actors at the state level and public colleges and 
24:665:51141:28:27:89:501:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:universities that tuition (price) may not serve as a plausible channel for adjustment to 
24:666:51230:27:28:89:501:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:demand shocks and other market forces. Indeed, the evidence from regressions of the 
24:667:51314:28:28:89:497:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tuition charged to in-state students by public colleges and universities on cohort size 
24:668:51402:28:27:89:503:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:points clearly in the other direction: a 10% increase in cohort size is associated with a 
24:669:51492:27:28:89:523:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:2.7% decrease in tuition at the state comprehensive schools and 4.2% decrease at flagship 
24:670:51582:28:27:89:513:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:institutions. As in previous specifications, these regressions include state and year fixed 
24:671:51674:27:28:89:522:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:effects. Since there were secular increases in tuition at state schools over the period under 
24:672:51768:28:28:89:513:129:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:study, the right way to think about these results is that they suggest that tuition rose less 
24:673:51862:28:8:89:524:101:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:

25:682:52123:0:28:89:506:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:rapidly in states that experienced population growth over the period in question.23 It is 
25:683:52213:28:28:89:494:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:only among the non-research private institutions that we see a positive link between 
25:684:52298:28:27:89:202:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:tuition and cohort size. 
25:685:52323:27:28:125:264:626:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Non-tuition revenue sources 
25:686:52351:28:27:125:518:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Beyond tuition levels, we consider the relationship between cohort size and other 
25:687:52433:27:28:89:515:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:sources of revenue, including federal support and appropriations from state sources. Not 
25:688:52522:28:28:89:500:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:surprisingly, variation in federal support to colleges and universities is quite inelastic 
25:689:52613:28:27:89:512:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:with changes in cohort size, with an elasticity of about 0.27 (0.19), net of state and year 
25:690:52705:27:28:89:498:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:fixed effects for the interval from 1950-1996.  State appropriations demonstrate a bit 
25:691:52792:28:27:89:490:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:more adjustment to cohort size, with an elasticity of 0.58 (0.11).24  This estimate is 
25:692:52879:27:28:89:502:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:remarkably similar to the estimates we report in Tables 2 and 3 of the effect of cohort 
25:693:52967:28:28:89:506:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:size on college completion rates, which is consistent with our notion that the source of 
25:694:53056:28:27:89:475:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:these effects is college resources.25  While we do not have a full series, it seems 
25:695:53140:27:28:89:505:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:implausible that private philanthropy would adjust perfectly to changes in cohort size. 
25:696:53228:28:27:89:504:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Given the overall enrollment elasticities in Table 2, it is clear that non-tuition revenue 
25:697:53319:27:28:89:408:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:sources per student decline in the face of increases in cohort size.  
25:698:53389:28:55:125:285:267:12:g_d0_f5:12:g_d0_f5:Differences by type of institution

25:700:53568:12:11:89:131:200:10:g_d0_f3:6:g_d0_f3:each state, leading to the estimate of each of the 22 Fourier components for each state and year.  23
25:701:53669:11:12:132:489:189:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3: When state employment is included in the regression, the effect of cohort size on tuition 
25:702:53760:12:11:89:515:177:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:approaches zero, with increases in state employment having a substantial negative effect on tuition prices. 
25:703:53868:11:11:89:523:166:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:These results would seem to confirm the notion that states tend to raise tuitions when state economies are in 
25:704:53978:11:7:89:234:155:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f4:trouble and state budgets are tight.   
25:705:54016:7:17:125:498:148:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:24 The measures of state appropriations used in these regressions are from the Department of 
25:706:54109:17:11:89:498:131:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:Education’s institutional survey of college and university finances which extends from 1950 to 1996.  
25:707:54211:11:12:89:519:120:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:Measures of state appropriations from state-based finance surveys, which are available only after 1960, are 
25:708:54319:12:11:89:426:108:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:strongly correlated and yield a slightly smaller elasticity with respect to cohort size. 
25:709:54408:11:11:89:509:97:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:  25 As these estimates suggest, a regression of the log of the number BA degrees awarded on the 
25:710:54505:11:12:89:511:86:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:log of state appropriations (including state and year effects), using cohort size as an instrument, yields an 
25:711:54615:12:0:89:410:74:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:estimated coefficient that is close to and not statistically distinguishable from 1. 
26:712:54702:0:28:125:506:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Variation in relative adjustments in resources and enrollment across institution 
26:713:54783:28:28:89:501:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:types is to be expected from the differences in the objective functions of colleges and 
26:714:54871:28:27:89:491:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:universities. Community colleges and four-year institutions with modest admission 
26:715:54953:27:28:89:512:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:requirements tend to place “access” at the center of their mission, attempting to provide 
26:716:55043:28:27:89:498:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:enrollment opportunities for all applicants who meet minimum qualifications. At the 
26:717:55127:27:28:89:511:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:other extreme, research universities and liberal arts colleges are likely to emphasize the 
26:718:55218:28:28:89:493:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:role of student quality and resources per student as they make choices at the margin 
26:719:55303:28:27:89:472:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:between quality and quantity. These considerations suggest that we should find 
26:720:55382:27:28:89:503:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:substantial accommodation among two-year institutions to changes in cohort size and 
26:721:55466:28:27:89:505:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:little accommodation (and, perhaps, a ratcheting up of selectivity) among universities. 
26:722:55554:27:28:125:485:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:The regression results in Table 4 show the elasticity of enrollment and BA 
26:723:55629:28:28:89:515:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:completion with respect to cohort size (measured at age 18) by type of institution. In the 
26:724:55720:28:27:89:490:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:public sector, we distinguish community colleges, flagship institutions (the highest 
26:725:55805:27:28:89:493:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ranked public university in the state), and other four-year institutions. In addition to 
26:726:55894:28:27:89:492:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:presenting the total for all private institutions, we distinguish the unique and highly 
26:727:55982:27:28:89:506:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:subsidized set of research universities and liberal arts colleges. At one extreme, cohort 
26:728:56072:28:28:89:505:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:size has a coefficient that is close to 1 at the two-year colleges, indicating that at these 
26:729:56165:28:27:89:500:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:institutions enrollment levels fluctuate markedly with changes in cohort size. Among 
26:730:56250:27:28:89:513:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:public institutions awarding at least a BA degree, the flagship institutions are much less 
26:731:56341:28:27:89:505:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:responsive in degrees awarded and enrollment to changes in cohort size than the other 
26:732:56427:27:28:89:506:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:four-year institutions. At the other extreme, the elasticity of enrollment with respect to 
26:733:56518:28:28:89:505:129:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:cohort size is close to zero at the private liberal arts colleges and universities. (If these 
26:734:56612:28:0:89:524:101:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:institutions are in fact operating in a national rather than regional market, then they would 
27:735:56708:0:28:89:499:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:not face demand shocks at the state level – e.g., if there is a 10% population shock in 
27:736:56796:28:28:89:509:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:New Jersey, the change in applications to Princeton would be appreciably less than the 
27:737:56883:28:27:89:262:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:change in applications to Rutgers.)  
27:738:56920:27:28:89:515:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3: The likelihood of completing college differs markedly with the institution where 
27:739:57001:28:27:89:464:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:an individual starts in the higher education pipeline, even after accounting for 
27:740:57082:27:28:89:513:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:composition differences between the student bodies. Perhaps the most visible margin of 
27:741:57169:28:28:89:508:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:differences in completion rates occurs between the two-year and four-year institutions. 
27:742:57257:28:27:89:523:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Reynolds (2006) finds that college students starting at two-year schools with the intention 
27:743:57349:27:28:89:490:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of completing a BA and observable characteristics paralleling students at four-year 
27:744:57433:28:27:89:510:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:schools are much less likely to complete the BA than their peers beginning at four-year 
27:745:57521:27:28:89:508:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:schools.26  The probability of completing a BA is 0.48 for men, relative to 0.62 among 
27:746:57608:28:28:89:503:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:students starting at four-year institutions, and 0.59 for women, relative to 0.76 among 
27:747:57696:28:27:89:513:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:students starting at four-year institutions.  It follows that part of the effect of cohort size 
27:748:57792:27:28:89:509:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:on college completion rates works through the effect of cohort size on the nature of the 
27:749:57881:28:27:89:521:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:institutions students attend, with larger cohorts pushing a higher share of college students 
27:750:57974:27:28:89:497:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:to community colleges and open-access four-year institutions which tend to be more 
27:751:58057:28:28:89:401:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f4:limited in resources per student than state flagship universities.   
27:752:58125:28:27:89:429:239:12:g_d0_f4:12:g_d0_f4:IV. Changes in College Preparedness and Compositional Changes 
27:753:58187:27:28:125:497:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Within states, large cohorts are less likely to complete college than relatively 
27:754:58268:28:27:89:505:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:small cohorts. As discussed, adjustments on the supply side of the market provide one 
27:755:58354:27:28:89:510:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:explanation for this result. An alternative explanation is that changes in the demand for 
27:756:58444:28:0:89:494:129:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:college may be reduced among relatively large cohorts if college preparation is also 
28:757:58531:0:28:89:468:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:linked to cohort size. At issue in this analysis is whether results we attribute to 
28:758:58615:28:28:89:493:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:adjustments on the supply-side of the higher education market are instead related to 
28:759:58700:28:27:89:509:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:demand-side explanations. Two related concerns surface. First, relatively large cohorts 
28:760:58788:27:28:89:489:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:may be distinguished by adverse demographic or economic shocks that have direct 
28:761:58868:28:27:89:499:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:effects on collegiate attainment. For example, if big cohorts are distinguished by low 
28:762:58955:27:28:89:509:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:parental education or large family size, such “compositional effects” might account for 
28:763:59043:28:28:89:506:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:reduced college completion rather than crowding out on the supply side of the market. 
28:764:59129:28:27:89:518:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Secondly, membership in a relatively large birth cohort may dilute educational resources 
28:765:59218:27:28:89:509:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:at the elementary and secondary levels, which would also reduce college preparedness. 
28:766:59304:28:27:89:497:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Both types of effects imply that the change in college demand may be far less than a 
28:767:59389:27:28:89:200:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:change in cohort size.  
28:768:59413:28:28:125:506:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:To understand how compositional factors such as race, parental education, and 
28:769:59491:28:27:89:502:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:family size are tied to cohort size, we examined 16 year-olds (as 18 year-olds are less 
28:770:59579:27:28:89:509:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:likely to appear with their parents in the data) and their parental characteristics by state 
28:771:59672:28:27:89:522:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:for the 1970, 1980, and 1990 decennial Census enumerations.  Regressions of a particular 
28:772:59761:27:28:89:514:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:parental demographic characteristic (e.g., share black or number of children) on the size 
28:773:59851:28:28:89:522:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of the 16-year-old population in a state and Census year, with year and state fixed effects, 
28:774:59944:28:27:89:507:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:provides an indication of how changes in cohort size are tied to observed demographic 
28:775:60030:27:28:89:468:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:characteristics. More children in a family imply fewer resources per child, and 
28:776:60110:28:27:89:520:184:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:educational attainment—both formal and informal—is likely to be negatively affected by 
28:777:60197:27:28:89:506:157:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:a decrease in the time and financial resources available to each child within the family 
28:778:60286:28:28:89:500:129:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(Becker, 1981; Willis, 1973). Similarly, other family characteristics such as maternal 
28:779:60373:28:7:89:524:101:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                                                                                                                 
28:780:60517:7:16:125:520:94:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:26 Reynolds’ finding using matching estimators echoes earlier work using regressions to adjust for 
28:781:60616:16:0:89:515:78:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:observable differences between those starting at two and four year schools  (Rouse (1995), Leigh and Gill 
29:782:60724:0:28:89:492:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:education, family structure, and race may affect the educational resources available 
29:783:60809:28:28:89:185:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:outside of schools.  
29:784:60830:28:27:125:476:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:For the most part, we find effects that are not statistically significant and 
29:785:60908:27:28:89:497:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:economically small. This applies to measures of family size, parental education, and 
29:786:60993:28:27:89:484:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ethnicity. When we weight by state size, the only significant effect is on Hispanic 
29:787:61077:27:28:89:515:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ethnicity, with a coefficient of .27 indicating that a 2.7% increase in the share of college-
29:788:61170:28:28:89:507:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:age students of Hispanic origin would follow a 10% increase in the size of the 16-year-
29:789:61257:28:27:89:499:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:old population. Even with the upper end estimates of the effect of Hispanic origin on 
29:790:61343:27:28:89:510:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:college completion, these compositional effects could not explain a sizable share of the 
29:791:61432:28:27:89:396:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:reduction of college completion with increases in cohort size.27 
29:792:61496:27:28:125:494:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:Individuals in relatively large cohorts may also face diminished resources in 
29:793:61574:28:28:89:472:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:elementary and secondary schools, with these resource effects reducing college 
29:794:61653:28:27:89:510:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:preparedness and completion. Examination of the link between resources per student at 
29:795:61739:27:28:89:472:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:the primary and secondary levels and cohort size helps to place this question in 
29:796:61820:28:27:89:524:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:perspective. First, resources per student are negatively affected by increases in cohort size 
29:797:61914:27:28:89:512:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:at the elementary and secondary levels. Using data for the 1970 to 1995 period, we find 
29:798:62002:28:28:89:522:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:that variation in K-12 enrollment and in average daily attendance is linked to lower levels 
29:799:62094:28:27:89:520:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:of teachers per student, with coefficients -0.18 (0.06) and -0.16 (0.05) respectively which 
29:800:62186:27:34:89:350:212:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:represent elasticities from the log-log specification.28  
29:801:62244:34:12:89:524:178:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:                                                                                                                                                 
29:802:62388:12:7:89:378:166:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:(2003), and Sandy, Gonzales and Hilmer, forthcoming) community.       
29:803:62458:7:16:125:516:159:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:27 Additional evidence suggests that the increase in the fraction of the population that is Hispanic 
29:804:62559:16:12:89:518:143:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:can explain little, if any, of the crowd out effects we estimate.  The association between population growth 
29:805:62668:12:11:89:480:131:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:and the growth in the share of the population that is Hispanic largely reflects secular trends in the 
29:806:62770:11:7:89:472:120:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:Southwest, trends that are controlled for in our specifications that include state-specific trends.  
29:807:62871:7:16:125:519:113:6:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:28 Because it is not possible to distinguish enrollments and class size accurately at the grade level, 
29:808:62974:16:11:89:493:97:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:year and state aggregates over all elementary and secondary (K-12) enrollment form the variables of 
29:809:63074:11:12:89:523:86:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:interest.  Data are from Statistics of State School Systems (1998) for 1970-1995. Average Daily Attendance 
29:810:63181:12:0:89:510:74:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:is from Table 13, Fall elementary and secondary enrollment is from Table 10, and teachers per student is 
30:811:63288:0:28:125:515:709:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:What effect might these changes in resources available to K-12 students have on 
30:812:63368:28:28:89:509:681:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:college achievement? While an increasing number of research papers find a significant 
30:813:63454:28:27:89:518:653:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:effect of class size on student achievement (e.g., Krueger, 1999), most of these estimates 
30:814:63545:27:28:89:506:626:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:measure the effect of lower class size in a single year on achievement. Because we are 
30:815:63632:28:27:89:492:598:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:interested in the cumulative effects of class size on educational attainment, existing 
30:816:63719:27:28:89:515:571:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:estimates of the effect of class size on test scores do not provide an appropriate basis for 
30:817:63812:28:28:89:482:543:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:inference. Instead, following Card and Krueger (1992), we use the average of the 
30:818:63893:28:27:89:515:515:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:potential pupil-teacher ratio faced by the student in a state. Using measures of collegiate 
30:819:63985:27:28:89:509:488:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:attainment from Census data, we find significant effects of the teacher-student ratio for 
30:820:64075:28:27:89:499:460:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:all Census years examined.29 The estimated effect of teacher-student ratio on college 
30:821:64161:27:28:89:503:433:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:completion for the 1926-1975 birth cohorts in the 2000 Census is large –  0.46 (0.15), 
30:822:64248:28:28:89:483:405:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:with both collegiate attainment and resources measured in logs.  Such evidence is 
30:823:64330:28:27:89:523:377:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:indicative of reductions in college preparedness associated with resource declines in large 
30:824:64422:27:28:89:508:350:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:cohorts, but likely represents an upper bound of the causal effect of the teacher student 
30:825:64512:28:27:89:503:322:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:ratio on attainment because state-level changes in the student-teacher ration are likely 
30:826:64601:27:28:89:494:295:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:correlated with other policy changes affecting attainment, including lengthening the 
30:827:64686:28:28:89:523:267:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:school year and investing in higher education.30 Still, combining the effects of enrollment 
30:828:64778:28:35:89:324:239:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:on resources and resources on degree attainment 
30:829:64826:35:10:89:93:204:12:g_d0_f3:12:g_d0_f3:(
